			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">2007–08 world food price crisis</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a>)</span></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">

</td>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_Food_Price_Index.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/FAO_Food_Price_Index.png/300px-FAO_Food_Price_Index.png" width="300" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/FAO_Food_Price_Index.png/450px-FAO_Food_Price_Index.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/FAO_Food_Price_Index.png/600px-FAO_Food_Price_Index.png 2x" data-file-width="3508" data-file-height="2480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_Food_Price_Index.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World food <a href="/wiki/Price_index" title="Price index">price index</a>, 1990–2012. Record high prices occurred during the food price crisis followed by another surge in prices since 2010. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the United Nations.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>World <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">food prices</a> increased dramatically in 2007 and the first and second quarter of 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-WFSFAO_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFSFAO-1">[1]</a></sup> creating a <a href="/wiki/International_crisis" title="International crisis">global crisis</a> and causing political and <a href="/wiki/Economic_stability" title="Economic stability">economic instability</a> and social unrest in both <a href="/wiki/Poor_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor countries">poor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed nations</a>. Although the media spotlight focused on the riots that ensued in the face of high prices, the ongoing crisis of <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food insecurity</a> has been years in the making.<sup id="cite_ref-ICTSD_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICTSD-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FAO2009_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO2009-3">[3]</a></sup> Systemic causes for the worldwide increases in food prices continue to be the subject of debate. After peaking in the second quarter of 2008, prices fell dramatically during the <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s recession">late-2000s recession</a> but increased during 2009 and 2010, peaking again in early 2011 at a level slightly higher than the level reached in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-WFSFAO_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFSFAO-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> However a repeat of the crisis of 2008 is not anticipated due to ample stockpiles.<sup id="cite_ref-FO11.2F10_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FO11.2F10-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The initial causes of the late-2006 price spikes included <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a> in grain-producing nations and <a href="/wiki/2000s_energy_crisis" title="2000s energy crisis">rising oil prices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Oil price increases also caused general escalations in the costs of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, food transportation, and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial agriculture">industrial agriculture</a>. Root causes may be the increasing use of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> in developed countries (see also <a href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food vs fuel">food vs fuel</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and an increasing demand for a more varied diet across the expanding middle-class populations of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC16Oct2008_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC16Oct2008-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> also raised concerns about the role of <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a> <a href="/wiki/Food_speculation" title="Food speculation">speculating</a> on prices leading to major shifts in prices.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> These factors, coupled with falling world-food stockpiles, all contributed to the worldwide rise in food prices.<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png/300px-Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png" width="300" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png/450px-Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png/600px-Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png 2x" data-file-width="655" data-file-height="548" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wheat_yields_in_developing_countries_1951-2004.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wheat yields in developing countries, 1950 to 2004, kg/HA baseline 500. The steep rise in <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> in the US began in the 1940s. The percentage of growth was fastest in the early rapid growth stage. In developed counties the yield growth slowdown has been less for maize than for wheat and soybeans. In developing countries maize yields are still rapidly rising.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Projections"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Projections</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#After_the_crisis"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">After the crisis</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Actions_by_governments"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Actions by governments</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#Food_price_decreases"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Food price decreases</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Drastic_price_increases">Drastic price increases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Drastic price increases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Between 2006 and 2008 average world prices for rice rose by 217%, wheat by 136%, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> by 125% and soybeans by 107%.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In late April 2008 rice prices hit 24 cents (US) per US pound, more than doubling the price in just seven months.<sup id="cite_ref-cyclone_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cyclone-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_population_growth">World population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: World population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/220px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/330px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/440px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Growth in food production was greater than population growth since 1961.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although some commentators have argued that this food crisis stems from unprecedented <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">global</a> <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> others point out that world population <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories by fertility rate">growth rates</a> have dropped dramatically since the 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and grain availability has continued to outpace population.</p>
<p>In order to prevent price growth, food production should outpace population growth, which <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">was about 1.2% per year</a>. But there was a temporary drop in food production growth:<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> for example, wheat production during 2006 and 2007 <a href="/wiki/International_wheat_production_statistics" title="International wheat production statistics">was 4% lower</a> than that in 2004 and 2005.</p>
<p>World population has grown from 1.6 billion in 1900 to over 7.2 billion today.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_demand_for_more_resource_intensive_food">Increased demand for more resource intensive food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Increased demand for more resource intensive food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The head of the <a href="/wiki/International_Food_Policy_Research_Institute" title="International Food Policy Research Institute">International Food Policy Research Institute</a>, stated in 2008 that the gradual change in diet among newly prosperous populations is the most important factor underpinning the rise in global food prices.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Where food consumption has increased, it has largely been in processed ("<a href="/wiki/Value_added" title="Value added">value added</a>") foods, sold in developing and developed nations.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Total grain utilization growth since 2006 (up three percent, over the 2000–2006 per annum average of two percent) has been greatest in non-food usage, especially in feed and biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>One kilogram of beef requires seven kilograms of feed grain.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> These reports, therefore, conclude that usage in industrial, feed, and input intensive foods, not population growth among poor consumers of simple grains, has contributed to the price increases. Rising meat consumption due to changes in lifestyle can in turn lead to higher energy consumption due to the higher energy-intensity of meat products, for example, one kilogram of meat uses about 19 times as much energy to produce it as the same amount of apple.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;margin:0 0 1em 1em;">
<caption>2005/1990 ratios of per capita consumption<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></caption>

</table>
<p>Although the vast majority of the population in Asia remains rural and poor, the growth of the middle class in the region has been dramatic. For comparison, in 1990, the middle class grew by 9.7 percent in India and 8.6 percent in China, but by 2007 the <a href="/wiki/Population_growth_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Population growth rate">growth rate</a> was nearly 30 percent and 70 percent respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup> The corresponding increase in Asian affluence also brought with it a change in lifestyle and eating habits, particularly a demand for greater variety, leading to increased competition with western nations for already strained agricultural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC16Oct2008_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC16Oct2008-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This demand exacerbates dramatic increases in <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a> prices such as oil.</p>
<p>Another issue with rising affluence in India and China was reducing the "shock absorber" of poor people who are forced to reduce their resource consumption when food prices rise. This reduced price elasticity and caused a sharp rise in food prices during some shortages. In the media, China is often mentioned as one of the main reasons for the increase in world food prices. However, China has to a large extent been able to meet its own demand for food, and even exports its surpluses in the world market.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_petroleum_and_fertilizer_price_increases">Effects of petroleum and fertilizer price increases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Effects of petroleum and fertilizer price increases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Starting in 2007, the prices of fertilizers of all kinds increased dramatically, peaking around the summer of 2008 (see <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.fertilizer.org/ifa/HomePage/FERTILIZERS-THE-INDUSTRY/Global-fertilizer-trade-flow-map">graphs</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Fertilizer_Industry_Association" title="International Fertilizer Industry Association">International Fertilizer Industry Association</a>). Prices approximately tripled for <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diammonium_phosphate" title="Diammonium phosphate">diammonium phosphate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muriate_of_potash" class="mw-redirect" title="Muriate of potash">muriate of potash</a> (KCl), and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (used for making phosphate fertilizer), and then fell just as dramatically in the latter part of 2008. Some prices doubled within the six months before April 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Part of the cause for these price rises was the <a href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_since_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil price increases since 2003">rise in the price of oil</a>, since the most fertilizers require petroleum or natural gas to manufacture.<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup> Although the main fossil fuel input for fertilizer comes from natural gas to generate hydrogen for the <a href="/wiki/Haber%E2%80%93Bosch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Haber–Bosch process">Haber–Bosch process</a> (see: <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">Ammonia production</a>), natural gas has its own supply problems similar to those for oil. Because natural gas can substitute for petroleum in some uses (for example, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural gas liquids">natural gas liquids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuel power plant">electricity generation</a>), increasing prices for petroleum lead to increasing prices for natural gas, and thus for fertilizer.</p>
<p>Costs for fertilizer raw materials other than oil, such as <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>, have been increasing<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> as increased production of staples increases demand. This is causing a boom (with associated volatility) in agriculture stocks.</p>
<p>The major <a href="/wiki/IFPRI" class="mw-redirect" title="IFPRI">IFPRI</a> Report launched in February 2011 stated that the causes of the 2008 global food crisis were similar to that of the 1972–74 food crisis, in that the oil price and energy price was the major driver, as well as the shock to cereal demand (from biofuels this time), low interest rates, devaluation of the dollar, declining stocks, and some adverse weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Unfortunately the IFPRI states that such shocks are likely to recur with several shocks in the future; compounded by a long history of neglecting agricultural investments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declining_world_food_stockpiles">Declining world food stockpiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Declining world food stockpiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the past, nations tended to keep more sizable food stockpiles, but more recently, due to a faster pace of food growth and ease of importation, less emphasis is placed on high stockpiles. For example, in February 2008 wheat stockpiles hit a 60-year low in the United States (see also <a href="/wiki/Rice_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Rice shortage">Rice shortage</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup> Data stocks are often calculated as a residual between Production and Consumption but it becomes difficult to discriminate between a de-stocking policy choices of individual countries and a deficit between production and consumption.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_speculation">Financial speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Financial speculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Destabilizing influences, including indiscriminate lending and real estate speculation, led to a <a href="/wiki/Black_Monday_(January_2008)" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Monday (January 2008)">crisis in January 2008</a>, and eroded investment in food commodities.<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Foreign investment drives productivity improvements, and other gains for <a href="/wiki/Farmer" title="Farmer">farmers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Commodity_index_funds">Commodity index funds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Commodity index funds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commodity_index_fund" title="Commodity index fund">Commodity index fund</a></div>
<p>Goldman Sachs' entry into the commodities market via the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_GSCI" title="S&amp;P GSCI">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index</a> has been implicated by some in the 2007–2008 world food price crisis. In a 2010 article in Harper's magazine, Frederick Kaufman magazine accused Goldman Sachs of profiting while many people went hungry or even starved. He argued that Goldman's large purchases of long-options on wheat futures created a <a href="/wiki/Demand_shock" title="Demand shock">demand shock</a> in the wheat market, which disturbed the normal relationship between <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a> and price levels. He argues that the result was a '<a href="/wiki/Contango" title="Contango">contango</a>' wheat market on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, which caused prices of wheat to rise much higher than normal, defeating the purpose of the exchanges (price stabilization) in the first place.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> however, a report by the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> – using data from the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> – showed tracking funds (of which Goldman Sachs Commodity Index was one) did not cause the bubble. For example, the report points out that even commodities without futures markets also saw price rises during the period.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Some commodities <i>without</i> futures markets saw their prices rise as a consequence of the rising prices of commodities <i>with</i> futures markets: the World Development Movement states there is strong evidence that the rising price of wheat caused the price of rice to subsequently rise as well.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_trade_liberalization">Effects of trade liberalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Effects of trade liberalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some theorists, such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Khor" title="Martin Khor">Martin Khor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Third_World_Network" title="Third World Network">Third World Network</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MK_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MK-42">[42]</a></sup> point out that many developing nations have gone from being food independent to being net food importing economies since the 1970s and 1980s <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (and later the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="World Trade Organisation">World Trade Organisation</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Agreement_on_Agriculture" title="Agreement on Agriculture">Agreement on Agriculture</a>) <a href="/wiki/Free_market_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Free market economics">free market economics</a> directives to debtor nations. In opening developing countries to <a href="/wiki/Developed_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Developed world">developed world</a> food imports subsidised by Western governments, <a href="/wiki/Developing_nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing nations">developing nations</a> can become more dependent upon food imports if local agriculture does not improve.<sup id="cite_ref-MK_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MK-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>While developed countries pressured the developing world to abolish subsidies in the interest of trade liberalization, rich countries largely kept subsidies in place for their own farmers. In recent years United States government subsidies have been added to push production toward biofuel rather than food and vegetables .<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_food_for_fuel">Effects of food for fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Effects of food for fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food vs fuel">Food vs fuel</a></div>
<p>One systemic cause for the price rise is held to be the diversion of food crops (maize in particular) for making <a href="/wiki/First-generation_biofuel" class="mw-redirect" title="First-generation biofuel">first-generation biofuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> An estimated 100 million tons of grain per year are being redirected from food to fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> (Total worldwide grain production for 2007 was just over 2000 million tons.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>) As farmers devoted larger parts of their crops to fuel production than in previous years, land and resources available for food production were reduced correspondingly.</p>
<p>This has resulted in less food available for human consumption, especially in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Least_developed_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Least developed countries">least developed countries</a>, where a family's daily allowances for food purchases are extremely limited. The crisis can be seen, in a sense, to dichotomize <a href="/wiki/North-South_divide" class="mw-redirect" title="North-South divide">rich and poor nations</a>, since, for example, filling a tank of an average car with <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>, amounts to as much maize (Africa's principal food staple) as an African person consumes in an entire year.<sup id="cite_ref-gm_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gm-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, the world's second largest producer of ethanol after the US, is considered to have the world's first sustainable biofuels economy<sup id="cite_ref-Apollo_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Econon06_08_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econon06_08-49">[49]</a></sup> and its government claims Brazil's sugar cane based ethanol industry has not contributed to the 2008 food crises.<sup id="cite_ref-Econon06_08_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econon06_08-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veja_30_04_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_30_04-50">[50]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> policy research working paper released in July 2008<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank_07_08_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank_07_08-51">[51]</a></sup> concluded that "...large increases in biofuels production in the United States and Europe are the main reason behind the steep rise in global food prices", and also stated that "Brazil's sugar-based ethanol did not push food prices appreciably higher".<sup id="cite_ref-Veja_28_07_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_28_07-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-53">[53]</a></sup> An economic assessment published in July 2008 by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a><sup id="cite_ref-OECD_07_08_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_07_08-54">[54]</a></sup> disagrees with the World Bank report regarding the negative effects of subsidies and trade restrictions, finding that the effect of biofuels on food prices are much smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-OECDweb08_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECDweb08-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>A report released by <a href="/wiki/Oxfam" title="Oxfam">Oxfam</a> in June 2008<sup id="cite_ref-Oxfampdf_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfampdf-56">[56]</a></sup> criticized biofuel policies of rich countries and concluded that, of all biofuels available in the market, Brazilian sugarcane ethanol is "far from perfect" but it is the most favorable biofuel in the world in term of cost and <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">GHG</a> balance. The report discusses some existing problems and potential risks and asks the Brazilian government for caution to avoid jeopardizing its environmental and social sustainability. The report also says that: "Rich countries spent up to $15 billion last year supporting biofuels while blocking cheaper Brazilian ethanol, which is far less damaging for global <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Oxfam08_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfam08-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Ethanol fuel in Brazil</a>)</p>
<p>German Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a> said the rise in food prices is due to poor agricultural policies and changing eating habits in developing nations, not biofuels as some critics claim.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> On 29 April 2008, US President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> declared during a press conference that "85 percent of the world's food prices are caused by weather, increased demand and energy prices", and recognized that "15 percent has been caused by ethanol".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> On 4 July 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that a leaked World Bank report estimated the rise in food prices caused by biofuels to be 75%.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This report was officially released in July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank_07_08_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank_07_08-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since reaching record high prices in June 2008, corn prices fell 50% by October 2008, declining sharply together with other <a href="/wiki/Commodities" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodities">commodities</a>, including oil. As ethanol production from corn has continued at the same levels, some have argued this trend shows the belief that the increased demand for corn to produce ethanol was mistaken. "Analysts, including some in the ethanol sector, say ethanol demand adds about 75 cents to $1.00 per bushel to the price of corn, as a rule of thumb. Other analysts say it adds around 20 percent, or just under 80 cents per bushel at current prices. Those estimates hint that $4 per bushel corn might be priced at only $3 without demand for ethanol fuel."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> These industry sources consider that a <a href="/wiki/Speculative_bubble" class="mw-redirect" title="Speculative bubble">speculative bubble</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_markets" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodity markets">commodity markets</a> holding positions in corn <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures</a> was the main driver behind the observed hike in corn prices affecting food supply.</p>
<p>Second- and third-generation biofuels (such as <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">algae fuel</a>, respectively) may someday ease the competition with food crops, as can grow on marginal lands unsuited for food crops, but these advanced biofuels require further development of farming practices and refining technology; in contrast, ethanol from maize uses <a href="/wiki/Mature_technology" title="Mature technology">mature technology</a> and the maize crop can be shifted between food and fuel use quickly.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuel_subsidies_in_the_US_and_the_EU">Biofuel subsidies in the US and the EU</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biofuel subsidies in the US and the EU">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The World Bank lists the effect of biofuels as an important contributor to higher food prices.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/FAO" class="mw-redirect" title="FAO">FAO</a>/ECMB has reported that world land usage for agriculture has declined since the 1980s, and subsidies outside the United States and EU have dropped since the year 2004, leaving supply, while sufficient to meet 2004 needs, vulnerable when the United States began converting agricultural commodities to <a href="/wiki/Biofuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> (USDA), global wheat imports and stocks have decreased, domestic consumption has stagnated, and world wheat production has decreased from 2006 to 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, government subsidies for ethanol production have prompted many farmers to switch to production for biofuel. Maize is the primary crop used for the production of ethanol, with the United States being the biggest producer of maize ethanol. As a result, 23 percent of United States maize crops were being used for ethanol in 2006–2007 (up from 6 percent in 2005–2006), and the USDA expects the United States to use 81 million tons of maize for ethanol production in the 2007–2008 season, up 37 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> This not only diverts grains from food, but it diverts agricultural land from food production.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, supporters of ethanol claim that using <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> for ethanol is not responsible for the worst <a href="/wiki/Food_riot" title="Food riot">food riots</a> in the world, many of which have been caused by the price of rice and oil, which are not affected by biofuel use but rather by supply and demand.</p>
<p>However, a World Bank policy research working paper released in July 2008<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank_07_08_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank_07_08-51">[51]</a></sup> says that <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> have raised food prices between 70 and 75 percent. The study found that higher oil prices and a weak dollar explain 25–30% of total price rise. The "month-by-month" five-year analysis disputes that increases in global grain consumption and droughts were responsible for price increases, reporting that this had had only a marginal effect and instead argues that the EU and US drive for biofuels has had by far the biggest effect on food supply and prices. The paper concludes that increased production of biofuels in the US and EU were supported by subsidies and tariffs on imports, and considers that without these policies, price increases would have been smaller. This research also concluded that <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>'s sugar cane based ethanol has not raised sugar prices significantly, and suggest to remove tariffs on ethanol imports by both the US and EU, to allow more efficient producers such as Brazil and other developing countries to produce ethanol profitably for export to meet the mandates in the EU and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-Veja_28_07_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_28_07-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Renewable Fuel Association (RFA) published a rebuttal based on the version leaked before the formal release of the World Bank's paper.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The RFA critique considers that the analysis is highly subjective and that the author "estimates the effect of global food prices from the weak dollar and the direct and indirect effect of high petroleum prices and attributes everything else to biofuels."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>An economic assessment report also published in July 2008 by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a><sup id="cite_ref-OECD_07_08_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_07_08-54">[54]</a></sup> agrees with the World Bank report regarding the negative effects of subsidies and trade restrictions, but found that the effect of biofuels on food prices are much smaller. The OECD study is also critical of the limited reduction of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">GHG</a> emissions achieved from biofuels produced in Europe and North America, concluding that the current biofuel support policies would reduce greenhouse gas emissions from transport fuel by no more than 0.8 percent by 2015, while Brazilian ethanol from sugar cane reduces greenhouse gas emissions by at least 80 percent compared to fossil fuels. The assessment calls on governments for more open markets in biofuels and feedstocks to improve efficiency and lower costs.<sup id="cite_ref-OECDweb08_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECDweb08-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Idled_farmland">Idled farmland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Idled farmland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <i>New York Times</i> on 9 April 2008, the United States government pays farmers to <a href="/wiki/Fallow" class="mw-redirect" title="Fallow">idle their cropland</a> under a conservation program. This policy reached a peak of 36,800,000 acres (149,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) idled in 2007, that is 8% of cropland in United States, representing a total area bigger than the state of New York.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_subsidies">Agricultural subsidies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Agricultural subsidies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The global food crisis has renewed calls for removal of distorting <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">agricultural subsidies</a> in developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Support to farmers in OECD countries totals 280 billion USD annually, which compares to official development assistance of just 80 billion USD in 2004, and farm support depresses global food prices, according to OECD estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidies" class="mw-redirect" title="Agricultural subsidies">agricultural subsidies</a> lead to underdevelopment in rural developing countries, including the <a href="/wiki/Least_developed_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Least developed countries">least developed countries</a>; meanwhile subsidised food increases overconsumption in developed countries. The US Farm Bill brought in by the Bush Administration in 2002 increased agricultural subsidies by 80% and cost the US taxpayer 190 billion USD.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In 2003, the EU agreed to extend the <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a> until 2013. Former UNDP Administrator Malloch Brown renewed calls for reform of the farm subsidies such as the CAP.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distorted_global_rice_market">Distorted global rice market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Distorted global rice market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Japan is forced to import more than 767,000 tons of rice annually from the United States, Thailand, and other countries due to <a href="/wiki/WTO" class="mw-redirect" title="WTO">WTO</a> rules,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> although Japan produces over 100% of domestic rice consumption needs with 11 million tons produced in 2005 while 8.7 million tons were consumed in 2003–2004 period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Japan is not allowed to re-export this rice to other countries without approval. This rice is generally left to rot and then used for <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">animal feed</a>. Under pressure, the United States and Japan are poised to strike a deal to remove such restrictions. It is expected 1.5 million tons of high-grade American rice will enter the market soon.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_shortfalls_from_natural_disasters">Crop shortfalls from natural disasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Crop shortfalls from natural disasters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several distinct weather- and climate-related incidents have caused disruptions in crop production. Perhaps the most influential is the <a href="/wiki/Drought_in_Australia#Rainfall_deficiencies_in_2006" title="Drought in Australia">extended drought in Australia</a>, in particular the fertile <a href="/wiki/Murray-Darling_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Murray-Darling Basin">Murray-Darling Basin</a>, which produces large amounts of wheat and rice. The drought has caused the annual rice harvest to fall by as much as 98% from pre-drought levels.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Australia is historically the second-largest exporter of wheat after the United States, producing up to 25 million tons in a good year, the vast majority for export. However, the 2006 harvest was 9.8 million.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Other events that have negatively affected the price of food include the <a href="/wiki/2006_North_American_heat_wave" title="2006 North American heat wave">2006 heat wave</a> in California's <a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_Valley" title="San Joaquin Valley">San Joaquin Valley</a>, which killed large numbers of farm animals, and unseasonable 2008 rains in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, India, which destroyed swathes of grain. These incidents are consistent with the effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The effects of <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Nargis" title="Cyclone Nargis">Cyclone Nargis</a> on <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a> in May 2008 caused a spike in the price of rice. Burma has historically been a rice exporter, though yields have fallen as government <a href="/wiki/Price_controls" title="Price controls">price controls</a> have reduced incentives for farmers. The <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a> inundated rice paddies up to 30 miles (48&#160;km) inland in the <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_Delta" title="Irrawaddy Delta">Irrawaddy Delta</a>, raising concern that the salt could make the fields infertile. The FAO had previously estimated that Burma would export up to 600,000 tons of rice in 2008, but concerns were raised in the cyclone's aftermath that Burma may be forced to import rice for the first time, putting further upward pressure on global rice prices.<sup id="cite_ref-cyclone_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cyclone-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stem_rust" title="Stem rust">Stem rust</a> reappeared in 1998<sup id="cite_ref-cornell_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-81">[81]</a></sup> in Uganda (and possibly earlier in Kenya)<sup id="cite_ref-CIMMYT_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIMMYT-82">[82]</a></sup> with the particularly virulent <a href="/wiki/Stem_rust#Ug99" title="Stem rust">UG99</a> fungus. Unlike other rusts, which only partially affect crop yields, UG99 can bring 100% crop loss. Up to 80% yield losses were recently recorded in Kenya.<sup id="cite_ref-KARI_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KARI-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2005 stem rust was still believed to be "largely under control worldwide except in Eastern Africa".<sup id="cite_ref-CIMMYT_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIMMYT-82">[82]</a></sup> But by January 2007 an even more virulent strain had gone across the Red Sea into Yemen. FAO first reported on 5 March 2008 that Ug99 had now spread to major wheat-growing areas in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-FAOmar08_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOmar08-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>These countries in North Africa and Middle East consume over 150% of their own wheat production;<sup id="cite_ref-cornell_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-81">[81]</a></sup> the failure of this staple crop thus adds a major burden on them. The disease is now expected to spread over China and the Far-East. The strong international collaboration network of research and development that spread disease-resistant strains some 40 years ago and started the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/CGIAR" title="CGIAR">CGIAR</a>, was since slowly starved of research funds because of its own success and is now too atrophied to swiftly react to the new threat.<sup id="cite_ref-cornell_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornell-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil_and_productivity_losses">Soil and productivity losses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Soil and productivity losses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sundquist<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> points out that large areas of croplands are lost year after year, due mainly to <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, water depletion and urbanisation. According to him "60,000 km<sup>2</sup>/year of land becomes so severely degraded that it loses its productive capacity and becomes wasteland", and even more are affected to a lesser extent, adding to the crop supply problem.</p>
<p>Additionally, agricultural production is also lost due to <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">water depletion</a>. Northern China in particular has depleted much of its non-renewables <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, which now impacts negatively its crop production.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Urbanisation is another, smaller, difficult to estimate cause of annual cropland reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rising_levels_of_ozone">Rising levels of ozone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rising levels of ozone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One possible environmental factor in the food price crisis is rising background levels of ground-level <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone" title="Tropospheric ozone">tropospheric ozone</a> in the atmosphere. Plants have been shown to have a high sensitivity to ozone levels, and lower yields of important food crops, such as wheat and soybeans, may have been a result of elevated ozone levels. Ozone levels in the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze Delta">Yangtze Delta</a> were studied for their effect on <a href="/wiki/Oilseed_rape" class="mw-redirect" title="Oilseed rape">oilseed rape</a>, a member of the cabbage family that produces one-third of the vegetable oil used in China. Plants grown in chambers that controlled ozone levels exhibited a 10–20 percent reduction in size and weight (<a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>) when exposed to elevated ozone levels. Production of seeds and oil was also reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Chinese authors of this study also reported that rice grown in chambers that controlled ozone levels exhibited a 14 to 20 percent reduction in <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> yield when exposed to ozone levels over 25 times higher than was normal for the region.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rising_prices">Rising prices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Rising prices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From the beginning of 2007 to early 2008, the prices of some of the most basic international food commodities increased dramatically on international markets.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The international market price of wheat doubled from February 2007 to February 2008 hitting a record high of over US$10 a <a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Rice prices also reached ten-year highs. In some nations, milk and meat prices more than doubled, while <a href="/wiki/Soy" class="mw-redirect" title="Soy">soy</a> (which hit a 34-year high price in December 2007<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>) and maize prices have increased dramatically.</p>
<p>Total food import bills rose by an estimated 25% for developing countries in 2007. Researchers from the <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> have suggested this problem will be worsened by a likely fall in food aid. As food aid is programmed by budget rather than volume, rising food prices mean that the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> (WFP) needs an extra $500 million just to sustain the current operations.<sup id="cite_ref-bpdnsw_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bpdnsw-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>To ensure that food remains available for their domestic populations and to combat dramatic price inflation, major rice exporters, such as China, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, India, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, Vietnam, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, imposed strict export bans on rice.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Conversely, several other nations, including <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, Ukraine, Russia, and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> either imposed high tariffs or blocked the export of wheat and other foodstuffs altogether, driving up prices still further for net food importing nations while trying to isolate their internal markets. <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> suffered from the food crisis to such extent that a North Korean official was quoted in June '08 with saying "Life is more than difficult. It seems that everyone is going to die".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-95">[95]</a></sup> This nation however is solely relying on food assistance to cope with the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_developed_countries">In developed countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=2007%E2%80%9308_world_food_price_crisis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In developed countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
